<p><sup id="cite_ref-DZ166_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DZ166-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DOS_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOS-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Universal_leadership">Universal leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Universal leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalkapitel">Generalkapitel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Generalkapitel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <i>Generalkapitel</i> (general chapter) was the collection of all the priests, knights and half-brothers (German: <i>Halbbrüder</i>). Because of the logistical problems in assembling the members, who were spread over large distances, only deputations of the <a href="/wiki/Bailiwick" title="Bailiwick">bailiwicks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commandry_(feudalism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Commandry (feudalism)">commandries</a> gathered to form the General chapter. The General chapter was designed to meet annually, but the conventions were usually limited to the election of a new Grandmaster. The decisions of the <i>Generalkapitel</i> had a binding effect on the <i>Großgebietigers</i> of the order.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hochmeister">Hochmeister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hochmeister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grand_Masters_of_the_Teutonic_Order" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Masters of the Teutonic Order">Grand Masters of the Teutonic Order</a></div>
<p>The <i>Hochmeister</i> (<a href="/wiki/Grandmaster_(order)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grandmaster (order)">Grandmaster</a>) was the highest officer of the order. Until 1525, he was elected by the <i>Generalkapitel</i>. He had the rank of an ecclesiastic <a href="/wiki/Imperial_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial state">imperial state</a> leader and was sovereign prince of Prussia until 1466. Despite this high formal position, practically, he only was a kind of <a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">first among equals</a>.</p>
<h5><span id="Großgebietiger"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gro.C3.9Fgebietiger">Großgebietiger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Großgebietiger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <i>Großgebietiger</i> were high officers with competence on the whole order, appointed by the <i>Hochmeister</i>. There were five offices.</p>
<ul>
<li>The <i>Großkomtur</i> (<i>Magnus Commendator</i>), the deputy of the Grandmaster</li>
<li>The <i>Treßler</i>, the treasurer</li>
<li>The <i>Spitler</i> (<i>Summus Hospitalarius</i>), responsible for all hospital affairs</li>
<li>The <i>Trapier</i>, responsible for dressing and armament</li>
<li>The <i>Marschall</i> (<i>Summus Marescalcus</i>), the chief of military affairs</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_leadership">National leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: National leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmeister">Landmeister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Landmeister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The order was divided in three national chapters, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Livland" class="mw-redirect" title="Livland">Livland</a> and the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire_of_the_German_Nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation">Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation</a>. The highest officer of each chapter was the <i>Landmeister</i> (country master). They were elected by the regional chapters. In the beginning, they were only substitutes of the Grandmaster but were able to create a power of their own so that, within their territory, the Grandmaster could not decide against their will. At the end of their rule over Prussia, the Grandmaster was only <i>Landmeister</i> of Prussia. There were three Landmeisters:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <i>Landmeister</i> in Livland, the successor of the <i>Herrenmeister</i> (lords master) of the former <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Brothers_of_the_Sword" title="Livonian Brothers of the Sword">Livonian Brothers of the Sword</a>.</li>
<li>The <i>Landmeister</i> of Prussia, after 1309 united with the office of the Grandmaster, who was situated in Prussia from then.</li>
<li>The <i>Deutschmeister</i>, the <i>Landsmeister</i> of the Holy Roman Empire. When Prussia and Livland were lost, the <i>Deutschmeister</i> also became Grandmaster.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_leadership">Regional leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Regional leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Because the properties of the order within the rule of the <i>Deutschmeister</i> did not form a contiguous territory, but were spread over the whole empire and parts of Europe, there was an additional regional structure, the bailiwick. <i>Kammerbaleien</i> were governed by the Grandmaster himself. Some of these bailiwicks had the rank of imperial states</p>
<ul>
<li>Deutschordensballei <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a> (Zwätzen)</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei <a href="/wiki/Hesse" title="Hesse">Hesse</a> (<a href="/wiki/Marburg" title="Marburg">Marburg</a>)</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxonia</a> (Lucklum)</li>
<li>Brandenburg</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei Westfalia (Deutschordenskommende <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BClheim" title="Mülheim">Mülheim</a>)</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei Franconia (<a href="/wiki/Ellingen" title="Ellingen">Ellingen</a>)</li>
<li>Kammerballei Koblenz</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a>-<a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a>-<a href="/wiki/Burgundy_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundy (region)">Burgundy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rouffach" title="Rouffach">Rouffach</a>)</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei at the Etsch and in the Mountains (south Tyrol) (<a href="/wiki/Bolzano" title="Bolzano">Bozen</a>)</li>
<li>Utrecht</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lotharingia" title="Lotharingia">Lorraine</a> (Trier)</li>
<li>Kammerballei <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a></li>
<li>Deutschordensballei Alden Biesen</li>
<li>Sicily</li>
<li>Deutschordensballei <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a> (San Leonardo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> (also called Lamparten)</li>
<li>Kammerballei <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a></li>
<li>Deutschordensballei Romania (Achaia, Greece)</li>
<li>Armenien-Zyprus</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_leadership">Local leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Local leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Komtur">Komtur</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Komtur">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The smallest administrative unit of the order was the <a href="/wiki/Kommende" class="mw-redirect" title="Kommende">Kommende</a>. It was ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Komtur" class="mw-redirect" title="Komtur">Komtur</a>, who had all administrative rights and controlled the <i>Vogteien</i> (district of a reeve) and <i>Zehnthöfe</i> (tithe collectors) within his rule. In the commandry, all kinds of brothers lived together in a monastic way. Noblemen served as Knight-brothers or Priest-brothers. Other people could serve as Sariantbrothers, who were armed soldiers, and as Half-brothers, who were working in economy and healthcare.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_offices">Special offices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Special offices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li>The <i>Kanzler</i> (chancellor) of the Grandmaster and the Deutschmeister. The chancellor took care of the keys and seals and was also the recording clerk of the chapter.</li>
<li>The <i>Münzmeister</i> (master of the mint) of Thorn. In 1246, the order received the right to produce its own coins - the <i>Moneta Dominorum Prussiae</i>&#160;– Schillingen.</li>
<li>The <i>Pfundmeister</i> (customs master) of Danzig. The <i>Pfund</i> was a local customs duty.</li>
<li>The <i>Generalprokurator</i> the representative of the order at the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>.</li>
<li>The <i>Großschäffer</i>, a trading representative with special authority.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_organisation">Modern organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Modern organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> order continued to exist in <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austria</a>, out of Napoleon's reach. From 1804 until 1923 (when <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Eugen_of_Austria" title="Archduke Eugen of Austria">Archduke Eugen of Austria</a> resigned the grandmastership), the order was headed by members of the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> dynasty. All the subsequent Grand Masters were priests.</p>
<p>In 1929, that branch of the Teutonic knights was converted to a purely spiritual <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Religious_order" title="Religious order">religious order</a> and renamed the <i>Deutscher Orden</i> ("German Order").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Nothing anywhere apparently dismisses the Order as a chivalric and aristocratic order, I'd like to see some sources (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Austria's annexation by Nazi Germany in 1938</a>, the Teutonic Order was suppressed throughout the <i>Großdeutsches Reich</i> until defeat of that regime, although the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazis</a> used imagery of the medieval Teutonic knights for propagandistic purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The Roman Catholic order survived in Italy, however, and was reconstituted in Germany and Austria in 1945.</p>
<p>By the end of the 20th century, this part of the Order had developed into a <a href="/wiki/Charitable_organization" title="Charitable organization">charitable organization</a> and established numerous clinics, as well as sponsoring excavation and tourism projects in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. In 2000, the German chapter of the Teutonic Order declared bankruptcy and its upper management was dismissed; an investigation by a special committee of the <a href="/wiki/Landtag_of_Bavaria" title="Landtag of Bavaria">Bavarian parliament</a> in 2002 and 2003 to determine the cause was inconclusive.</p>
<p>The Catholic branch now consists of approximately 1,000 members, including 100 <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a>, 200 <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a>, and 700 associates. While the priests are organized into six provinces (<a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, Germany, Italy, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>) and predominantly provide spiritual guidance, the nuns primarily care for the ill and the aged. Associates are active in Austria, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, the Czech Republic, Germany, and Italy. Many of the priests care for German-speaking communities outside of Germany and Austria, especially in Italy and Slovenia; in this sense the Teutonic Order has returned to its 12th-century roots: the spiritual and physical care of Germans in foreign lands.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The current General <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">Abbot</a> of the Order, who also holds the title of Grand Master, is <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Platter" title="Bruno Platter">Bruno Platter</a>.</p>
<p>The current seat of the Grand Master is the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Teutonic_Order,_Vienna" title="Church of the Teutonic Order, Vienna"><i>Deutschordenskirche</i></a> ("Church of the German Order") in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Near the <a href="/wiki/Stephansdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephansdom">Stephansdom</a> in the Austrian capital is the Treasury of the Teutonic Order, which is open to the public, and the Order's Central Archive. Since 1996, there has also been a museum dedicated to the Teutonic Knights at their former castle in Bad Mergentheim in Germany, which was the seat of the Grand Master from 1525–1809.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honorary_Knights">Honorary Knights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Honorary Knights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Honorary_Knights_of_the_Teutonic_Order" title="Category:Honorary Knights of the Teutonic Order">Category:Honorary Knights of the Teutonic Order</a></div>
<p>Honorary Knights of the Teutonic Order include <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Habsburg" title="Otto von Habsburg">Otto von Habsburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer" title="Konrad Adenauer">Konrad Adenauer</a>, and others.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Insignia">Insignia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Insignia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Knights wore white surcoats with a black cross, granted by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Innocent III</a> in 1205. A <a href="/wiki/Cross_patt%C3%A9e" title="Cross pattée">cross pattée</a> was sometimes used.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Need the year this event took place (February 2015)">year&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The motto of the Order was "Helfen, Wehren, Heilen" ("Help, Defend, Heal").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Need the year this event took place (February 2015)">year&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Demel_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demel-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The coat of arms representing the grand master (<i>Deutschmeisterwappen</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> is shown with a golden <a href="/wiki/Cross_fleury" title="Cross fleury">cross fleury</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cross_potent" title="Cross potent">cross potent</a> superimposed on the black cross, with the <a href="/wiki/Reichsadler" title="Reichsadler">imperial eagle</a> as a central inescutcheon. The golden cross fleury overlaid on the black cross became widely used in the 15th century. A legendary account attributes its introduction to <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX of France</a>, who on 20 August 1250 granted the master of the order this cross as a variation of the <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_cross" title="Jerusalem cross">Jerusalem cross</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Fleur-de-lis" title="Fleur-de-lis">fleur-de-lis</a> symbol attached to each arm. While this legendary account cannot be traced back further than the early modern period (Christoph Hartknoch, 1684), there is some evidence that the design does indeed date to the mid 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The black cross pattée was later used for military decoration and insignia by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a> and Germany as the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Cross" title="Iron Cross">Iron Cross</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pour_le_M%C3%A9rite" title="Pour le Mérite">Pour le Mérite</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_Bailiwick_of_Utrecht">Protestant Bailiwick of Utrecht</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Protestant Bailiwick of Utrecht">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A portion of the Order retains more of the character of the knights during the height of its power and prestige. Der <i>Balije van Utrecht</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Bailiwick_of_Utrecht" title="Bailiwick of Utrecht">Bailiwick of Utrecht</a>") of the <i>Ridderlijke Duitsche Orde</i> ("Chivalric German [i.e., 'Teutonic'] Order") became <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>, and it remained an aristocratic society. The relationship of the Bailiwick of Utrecht to the Roman Catholic <i>Deutscher Orden</i> resembles that of the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_John_(Bailiwick_of_Brandenburg)" title="Order of Saint John (Bailiwick of Brandenburg)">Bailiwick of Brandenburg</a> to the Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Malta" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Malta">Order of Malta</a>: each is an authentic part of its original order, though differing from and smaller than the Roman Catholic branch.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_German_and_Polish_nationalism">Influence on German and Polish nationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Teutonic_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Influence on German and Polish nationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_(1920).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_%281920%29.jpg/220px-German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_%281920%29.jpg" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_%281920%29.jpg/330px-German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_%281920%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_%281920%29.jpg/440px-German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_%281920%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="770" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:German_National_People%27s_Party_Poster_Teutonic_Knights_(1920).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/German_National_People%27s_Party" title="German National People's Party">German National People's Party</a> poster from 1920 showing a Teutonic knight being attacked by Poles and socialists. The caption reads "Save the East".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The German historian <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Treitschke" title="Heinrich von Treitschke">Heinrich von Treitschke</a> used imagery of the Teutonic Knights to promote pro-German and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Polish_sentiment" title="Anti-Polish sentiment">anti-Polish rhetoric</a>. Many middle-class German nationalists adopted this imagery and its symbols. During the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>, associations and organisations of this nature contributed to laying the groundwork for the formation of Nazi Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Mowia_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowia-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (July 2014)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Before and during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_propaganda" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi propaganda">Nazi propaganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">ideology</a> made frequent use of the Teutonic Knights' imagery, as the Nazis sought to depict the Knights' actions as a forerunner of the Nazi conquests for <a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a>. <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> tried to idealise the <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">SS</a> as a 20th-century re-incarnation of the medieval Order.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Yet, despite these references to the Teutonic Order's history in Nazi propaganda, the Order itself was abolished in 1938 and its members were persecuted by the German authorities. This occurred mostly due to Hitler's and Himmler's belief that, throughout history, Roman Catholic military-religious orders had been tools of the Holy See and as such constituted a threat to the Nazi regime.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The converse was true for Polish <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> (see: <a href="/wiki/Sienkiewicz" class="mw-redirect" title="Sienkiewicz">Sienkiewicz</a> "<a href="/wiki/The_Knights_of_the_Cross" title="The Knights of the Cross">The Knights of the Cross</a>"), which used the Teutonic Knights as symbolic shorthand for Germans in general, conflating the two into an easily recognisable image of the hostile. Similar associations were used by Soviet propagandists, such as the Teutonic knight villains in the 1938 <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Eisenstein" title="Sergei Eisenstein">Sergei Eisenstein</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nevsky_(film)" title="Alexander Nevsky (film)">Aleksandr Nevskii</a></i>.</p>
<p>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Wilhelm II</a> of Germany posed for a photo in 1902 in the garb of a monk from the Teutonic Order, climbing the stairs in the reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Malbork_Castle" title="Malbork Castle">Marienburg Castle</a> as a symbol of Imperial German policy.<sup id="cite_ref-Mowia_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowia-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (July 2014)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.deutscher-orden.de/GB/all_geschichte_start.php#aufstieg">"History of the German Order"</a>. <i>Teutonic Order, Order of the Teutonic Knights of St. Mary's Hospital in Jerusalem</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-01-30</span></span>. <q>This tradition was further perverted by the Nazis who, after the occupation of Austria suppressed it by an act of 6 September 1938 because they suspected it of being a bastion of pro-Habsburg legitimism.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATeutonic+Order&amp;rft.atitle=The+Teutonic+Order+of+Holy+Mary+in+Jerusalem&amp;rft.aufirst=Guy+Stair&amp;rft.aulast=Sainty&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Almanach+de+la+Cour&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.chivalricorders.org%2Fvatican%2Fteutonic.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-DOS-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-DOS_32-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ordensstaat.de/orden/sr.htm">Der Deutschordensstaat</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Sainty, Guy Stair. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.chivalricorders.org/vatican/teutonic.htm">"The Teutonic Order of Holy Mary in Jerusalem"</a>. <i>Almanach de la Cour</i>. www.chivalricorders.org<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-01-30</span></span>. <q>[T]he nazis...after the occupation of Austria suppressed [the Order] by an act of 6 September 1938 because they suspected it of being a bastion of pro-Habsburg legitimism. On occupying Czechoslovakia the following year, it was also suppressed in <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a> although the hospitals and houses in Yugoslavia and south Tyrol were able to continue a tenuous existence. The Nazis, motivated by Himmler's fantasies of reviving a German military elite then attempted to establish their own "Teutonic Order" as the highest award of the Third Reich. The ten recipients of this included Reinhard Heydrich and several of the most notorious Nazi criminals. Needless to say, although its badge was modeled on that of the genuine Order, it had absolutely nothing in common with it.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATeutonic+Order&amp;rft.atitle=The+Teutonic+Order+of+Holy+Mary+in+Jerusalem&amp;rft.aufirst=Guy+Stair&amp;rft.aulast=Sainty&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Almanach+de+la+Cour&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.chivalricorders.org%2Fvatican%2Fteutonic.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Urban, p. 277</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Deutschordenskirche, Wien 1</i> – an explanatory pamphlet (in German) of the Order available in the Deutschordenskirche, by Franz R. Vorderwinkler, 1996, published by Kirche &amp; Kultur Verlag mediapress, A-4400, Steyr.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The offices of <i>Hochmeister</i> (grand master, head of the order) and <i>Deutschmeister</i> (<i>Magister Germaniae</i>) were united in 1525. The title of <i>Magister Germaniae</i> had been introduced in 1219 as the head of the bailiwicks in the Holy Roman Empire, from 1381 also those in Italy, raised to the rank of a prince of the Holy Roman Empire in 1494, but merged with the office of grand master under Walter von Cronberg in 1525, from which time the head of the order had the title of <i>Hoch- und Deutschmeister</i>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.dr-bernhard-peter.de/Heraldik/Galerien2/galerie1533.htm">Bernhard Peter (2011)</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Helmut Nickel, "Über das Hochmeisterwappen des Deutschen Ordens im Heiligen Lande", <i>Der Herold</i> 4/1990, 97–108 (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mgh-bibliothek.de/dokumente/b/b038670.pdf">mgh-bibliothek.de</a>). Marie-Luise Heckmann, "Überlegungen zu einem heraldischen Repertorium an Hand der Hochmeisterwappen des Deutschen Ordens" in: Matthias Thumser, Janusz Tandecki, Dieter Heckmann (eds.) <i>Edition deutschsprachiger Quellen aus dem Ostseeraum (14.-16. Jahrhundert)</i>, Publikationen des Deutsch-Polnischen Gesprächskreises für Quellenedition. Publikacje Niemiecko-Polskiej Grupy Dyskusyjnej do Spraw Edycij Zrodel 1, 2001, 315–346 (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.hs-augsburg.de/~harsch/germanica/Chronologie/14Jh/GoldeneBulle/Wappen.htm">online edition</a>). "Die zeitgenössische Überlieferung verdeutlicht für dieses Wappen hingegen einen anderen Werdegang. Der Modelstein eines Schildmachers, der unter Hermann von Salza zwischen 1229 und 1266 auf der Starkenburg (<a href="/wiki/Montfort_Castle" title="Montfort Castle">Montfort</a>) im Heiligen Land tätig war, und ein rekonstruiertes Deckengemälde in der Burgkapelle derselben Festung erlaubten der Forschung den Schluss, dass sich die Hochmeister schon im 13. Jahrhundert eines eigenen Wappens bedient hätten. Es zeigte ein auf das schwarze Ordenskreuz aufgelegtes goldenes Lilienkreuz mit dem bekannten Adlerschildchen. Die Wappensiegel des Elbinger Komturs von 1310 bzw. 1319, ein heute in Innsbruck aufbewahrter Vortrageschild des Hochmeisters Karl von Trier von etwa 1320 und das schlecht erhaltene Sekretsiegel desselben Hochmeisters von 1323 sind ebenfalls jeweils mit aufgelegtem goldenem Lilienkreuz ausgestattet."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In this example (dated 1594), Hugo Dietrich von Hohenlandenberg, <a href="/wiki/Komtur" class="mw-redirect" title="Komtur">commander</a> of the bailiwick of Swabia-Alsace-Burgundy, shows his <a href="/wiki/Landenberg" title="Landenberg">Landenberg</a> family arms quartered with the order's black cross.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ridderlijkeduitscheorde.nl/">Official website of the Bailiwick of Utrecht, accessed March 15, 2010</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Mowia-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mowia_40-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mowia_40-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="languageicon">(in Polish)</span> <i>Mówią wieki</i>. "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.historia.terramail.pl/prasa/mowia_wieki_-_biala_legenda_czarnego_krzyza.pdf">Biała leganda czarnego krzyża</a>". Accessed 6 June 2006.</span></li>
